## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: classical mechanics — nonlinear oscillations / dynamical systems
**Analysis**: The target dv_dt is acceleration, which in mechanics is typically the restoring-force term (plus possible forcing) in a second-order oscillator. With available variables x and t, the most likely structures are conservative nonlinear restoring forces dv_dt = f(x) and/or explicitly time-periodic forcing dv_dt = f(x) + g(t).

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Linear harmonic oscillator (Hooke restoring): dv_dt = c0 * x (small-displacement restoring acceleration; sign absorbed into c0)
- Constant bias / gravity-like offset: dv_dt = c0 + c1 * x (equilibrium shifted by constant acceleration)
- Cubic (Duffing) nonlinearity: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 (hardening/softening spring restoring force)
- Quintic stiffness correction: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * x**5 (higher-order elastic nonlinearity)
- Odd polynomial restoring (symmetry about x=0): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * x**7 (generic symmetric nonlinear spring)
- Softening/saturating stiffness via rational form: dv_dt = c0 * x / (c1 + x**2) (restoring weakens at large |x|; bounded acceleration growth)
- Nonlinear pendulum restoring (exact): dv_dt = c0 * sin(c1 * x) (pendulum-like restoring when x is an angle; periodic in x)
- Periodic-in-x potential (washboard/Josephson-like): dv_dt = c0 * sin(c1 * x) + c2 * sin(c3 * x) (multi-harmonic periodic potential)
- Piecewise/impact-like stiffening using absolute value: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x * Abs(x) (non-smooth stiffness; different slope for ±x)
- Nonlinear stiffness with sign-dependent term: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * Abs(x) (asymmetric/non-odd restoring acceleration)
- Time-periodic external forcing (driven oscillator): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * cos(c2 * t) (sinusoidal drive added to restoring)
- Multi-tone forcing: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * cos(c2 * t) + c3 * cos(c4 * t) (two-frequency drive)
- Parametric excitation (time-varying stiffness): dv_dt = (c0 + c1 * cos(c2 * t)) * x (Mathieu-type parametric modulation)
- Driven Duffing form (nonlinear + forcing): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * cos(c3 * t) (canonical nonlinear forced oscillator)
- Nonlinear stiffness with parametric modulation: dv_dt = (c0 + c1 * cos(c2 * t)) * x + c3 * x**3 (Duffing–Mathieu hybrid structure)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Conservative oscillator signature: dv_dt depends primarily on x (i.e., dv_dt ≈ f(x)), indicating acceleration derives from a potential U(x) with dv_dt = -dU/dx (up to constants/sign).
- Symmetry about the origin: if the physics is symmetric under x → -x, dv_dt is typically an odd function of x (terms like x, x**3, sin(c*x)); even-in-x terms suggest asymmetry/bias.
- Hardening vs softening spring: adding +c*x**3 (with appropriate sign) increases effective stiffness with amplitude (hardening); the opposite sign decreases it (softening), often visible as amplitude-dependent frequency.
- Multi-well / bistability: inclusion of both linear and cubic terms with opposing signs (e.g., c0*x + c1*x**3 with c0 and c1 opposite-signed) implies a double-well potential and possible switching between equilibria.
- Bounded or saturating restoring acceleration: rational forms like x/(c + x**2) encode reduced incremental stiffness at large |x|, preventing acceleration magnitude from growing unboundedly.
- Periodic-in-x restoring: sin(c*x) indicates an angle-like coordinate or motion in a periodic potential; repeated equilibria spaced by 2π/c are expected.
- External periodic forcing: additive cos(c*t) terms suggest a driven oscillator; resonant response occurs when the forcing frequency matches (or is near) the natural frequency.
- Parametric modulation: multiplicative (c0 + c1*cos(c2*t))*x indicates time-varying stiffness; instability tongues (parametric resonance) can appear near c2 ≈ 2*natural frequency.
- Non-smooth mechanics: Abs(x) or x*Abs(x) structures indicate piecewise stiffness/clearance/impact-like behavior, often producing harmonics and sharp changes near x=0.

GT: F0*sin(t) - omega0**c0*(gamma*t + c1)*x - omega0**c2*x**c3 - omega0**c4*x
Candidate formulas: 50

GT (fixed) is
\[
f(x,t)= -1.99164100804766\,x^3\;-\;1.99164100804766\,x(0.0248350833882069\,t+1)\;-\;1.99164100804766\,x\;+\;0.799842060559254\sin t.
\]
Rewrite the linear-in-\(x\) part:
\[
-1.99164\,x(0.024835\,t+1) -1.99164\,x = -1.99164\,(0.024835)\,t\,x - 3.98328\,x,
\]
so GT contains the distinctive term **\(t\cdot x\)** (bilinear in \(t\) and \(x\)), plus an \(x^3\) term, plus a pure \(\sin(t)\) forcing, and no other \(t\)-dependence.

All candidates are built from combinations of:
- polynomial-in-\(x\) terms (e.g., \(x, x^3, x^5,\dots\)),
- time-only trig terms (\(\cos(\cdot)\), \(\sin(\cdot)\)),
- products like \(x\cos(\cdot)\), \(x e^{c t}\), \(e^{ct}\cos(\cdot)\),
- and sometimes \(\mathrm{Abs}(x)\) or rational-in-\(x\) terms.

Key obstruction: **None of the candidate families contains a term proportional to \(t\,x\)**. The only \(t\)-dependence appears inside trig functions or exponentials, never as a multiplicative factor \(t\) outside those functions. Because \(x\) can take any real value and \(t\) varies continuously, you cannot reproduce a globally linear-in-\(t\) coefficient multiplying \(x\) using a finite sum of exponentials/trig terms times \(x\) (unless you allow series/limits, which is not allowed here—only parameter choices).

Also, GT has \(\sin(t)\) (not \(\cos\)), but that part *could* be matched by phase-shifting a cosine: \(\sin t = \cos(t-\pi/2)\). So the forcing term alone is not an issue. The fatal issue is the **\(t x\)** term.

Now candidate-by-candidate:

1. \(c0 x + c1 x^3 +\) (time-only cos terms) \(+\;c4 x\cos(c5 t)+c9\).
   - Can match \(x^3\) via \(c1=-1.99164\).
   - Can match \(\sin t\) via a cosine with phase shift (use \(c6\cos(c7 t+c8)\) with \(c7=1, c8=-\pi/2, c6=0.799842\)).
   - But \(x\cos(c5 t)\) cannot equal \((\text{const}\cdot t)x\) for all \(t\). Setting \(c4=0\) removes it, but then there is no way to create the required \(t x\) term. **No match.**

2. Same as (1) plus \(c9 x^5\).
   - Extra \(x^5\) can be zeroed (set \(c9=0\)).
   - Still no \(t x\). **No match.**

3. Same as (1) plus \(c9\, x/(x^2+1)\).
   - Rational term cannot appear in GT; must set \(c9=0\).
   - Still no \(t x\). **No match.**

4. Has \(x\mathrm{Abs}(x)\) and cos terms.
   - \(x\mathrm{Abs}(x)\) is not globally equal to \(x^2\) with sign? It equals \(x|x|\), which is not \(x^3\) and not present in GT; must set its coefficient 0.
   - Still no \(t x\). **No match.**

   - Can match \(\sin t\) directly (set \(c7=0.799842, c8=1, c9=0\)) and kill cos term(s).
   - Linear \(x\) term can match \(-3.98328 x\) by choosing \(c0+c4=-3.98328\).
   - But again \(x\cos(c6 t)\) cannot produce \(t x\); if set \(c5=0\), no \(t x\) remains. **No match.**

   - Thus cannot generate the required bilinear term. **No match.**

Conclusion: every candidate lacks the essential bilinear term \((\text{const})\,t\,x\). Since parameters are constants (not functions of \(t\)), no parameter choice can make any candidate globally equal to the GT.

I need to analyze the Ground Truth (GT) and compare each candidate to it.

## GT Analysis

The GT numerical expression is:
$$-1.99164x^3 - 1.99164x(0.02484t + 1) - 1.99164x + 0.79984\sin(t)$$

Let me expand this:
$$-1.99164x^3 - 1.99164 \cdot 0.02484 \cdot tx - 1.99164x - 1.99164x + 0.79984\sin(t)$$
$$= -1.99164x^3 - 0.04947tx - 3.98328x + 0.79984\sin(t)$$

So the GT has these terms:
1. **$x^3$ term**: $-1.99164x^3$
2. **$tx$ term**: $-0.04947tx$
3. **$x$ term**: $-3.98328x$
4. **$\sin(t)$ term**: $0.79984\sin(t)$

The key basis functions are: $x$, $x^3$, $tx$, and $\sin(t)$.

Now I need to check which candidates can reproduce these terms. The critical question is whether each candidate has the ability to produce a **$tx$** term and a **$\sin(t)$** term (noting $\sin(t) = \cos(t - \pi/2)$).

**Key observations:**
- $\sin(t)$ can be written as $\cos(t + c)$ with appropriate phase shift, so any $\cos(ct + c)$ term can produce $\sin(t)$.
- The $tx$ term is the hardest to match. Let me check what structures can produce it.

**Can $x\cos(ct)$ produce $tx$?** No. $\cos$ is periodic, $t$ is linear. These are linearly independent.

**Can $x\exp(ct)$ produce $tx$?** Only if $c=0$, but then $\exp(0)=1$, giving just $x$, not $tx$. The exponential and linear functions are linearly independent, so no.

**Can $x \cdot \text{anything}(t)$ produce $tx$?** Only if that function of $t$ includes $t$ itself. $\cos(ct)$ cannot equal $t$. $\exp(ct)$ cannot equal $t$.

**Can $x\cos(ct)$ approximate $tx$ on a bounded domain?** No — we need EXACT equivalence, not approximation.

So the critical requirement is: the candidate must have a term that can produce $tx$. None of the candidates explicitly have a $tx$ term.

Wait — let me reconsider. Can we set $c5=0$ in $x\cos(c5\cdot t)$? Then $\cos(0) = 1$, giving just $x$. That doesn't help with $tx$.

Can $x\exp(c5\cdot t)$ with any constant produce $tx$? No, $e^{ct} \neq at+b$ for any constants.

**Conclusion:** None of the candidates contain a term that can produce $tx$. The GT requires a $tx$ term (linear coupling between $t$ and $x$), which is a basis function not present in any candidate.

Let me verify this is truly needed: the coefficient of $tx$ is $-0.04947$, which is small but nonzero. Since we need EXACT equivalence, this term cannot be dropped.

Therefore, **no candidate matches**.

Let me do a final sanity check on each candidate type to make sure I haven't missed anything creative... No, all candidates use combinations of $x$, $x^3$, $\cos$, $\exp$, $|x|$, $x^n$ terms — none can produce $tx$.
